SÄriputta, it is just like when various 
flowers are set down on a surface and are 
well held together with thread; the wind 
does not scatter, disperse, and separate 
them. For what reason? Because they are 
well held together with thread.


4.0

PREFACE
his volume is a concise guide to the BhikÂ­
khu PÄá¹­imokkha based on the Buddhist MoÂ­
nastic Code 1 (bmc1) by á¹¬hÄnissaro Bhikkhu. 
I have tried to distill the content of bmc1 to 
provide the reader with the most essential 
information on each PÄá¹­imokkha rule in a 
format that is clear and easy to understand.
Â¶Although the focus of this work is bmc1, which deals primarÂ­
ily with the Sutta Vibhaá¹…ga (vibh), I incorporate material from 
Buddhist Monastic Code 2 (bmc2), which deals primarily with the 
Khandhakas (kdks). This is most evident in Appendices 1 & 5, 
where the discussions of edibles and penance & probation are 
supplemented signiï¬cantly with material from bmc2.
Â¶In addition to using bmc1 and bmc2 as the basis of this work, 
I incorporate information from other English translations of 
the Bhikkhu PÄá¹­imokkha or the vibh. These include SuddhÄso 
Bhikkhuâ€™s Analysis of the Bhikkhu PÄtimokkha: a translation of 
the MahÄ-Vibhaá¹…ga in the Vinaya-Piá¹­aka (abp), I.B. Hornerâ€™s The 
Book of the Discipline (bd), K.R. Normanâ€™s The PÄtimokkha (pÄt), 
and Bhikkhu Ã‘Äá¹‡atusitaâ€™s A Translation and Analysis of the 
PÄtimokkha (tap).
Â¶I have tried to give a clear and accurate account of the sources 
for the information in bmc1. As sources arenâ€™t always clearly 
stated in bmc1, it is sometimes diï¬ƒcult to diï¬€erentiate beÂ­
tween what the source material says and what bmc1 adds in 
its interpretations of itâ€”which makes it diï¬ƒcult to ascertain 
the level of authority some of the information should be givÂ­
en. Information herein that is quoted or paraphrased from the 
vibh with negligible alteration (and is cited as such in bmc1) 
isnâ€™t preceded with any citation except in instances in which it 
was necessary to avoid ambiguity. Information from all other 
sources, including the kdks and ParivÄra, is preceded by the 
appropriate citationâ€”and statements that are explicitly stated 
in bmc1 as opinion, or donâ€™t appear to be directly based on any 
outside textual source, or are based on an argument from ignoÂ­
rance (i.e., based on the absence of textual evidence to the conÂ­
trary), or are statements for which I couldnâ€™t ï¬nd the source, 
are indicated with the citation, bmc1.
Â¶I have also tried to present the information in bmc1 as it is 
stated there, with minimal additions or commentary. The exÂ­
Prefaceâ€‚|â€‚e


ceptions are instances in which bmc1 omits signiï¬cant inforÂ­
mation in the vibh, or misrepresents the vibh (e.g., Pc 38), or 
presents questionable arguments to justify its interpretations 
(e.g., Pc 5, Pc 6, and its treatment of gelatin and miso). These 
and other additions and comments are preceded with the citaÂ­
tion, cbmc1.
Â¶I have adopted bmc1â€™s system of analyzing actions in terms 
of ï¬ve factors: eï¬€ort, perception, intention, object, and result. 
All ï¬ve factors arenâ€™t necessarily present in the analysis of evÂ­
ery ruleâ€”for some rules, all ï¬ve factors are relevant, in other 
rules, only one. The factors that arenâ€™t discussed are irrelevant 
to the analysis of the oï¬€ense. There are also, when appropriÂ­
ate, separate sections for derived oï¬€enses, non-oï¬€enses, related 
oï¬€enses, and related allowances. The only signiï¬cant deviaÂ­
tions from this schema are in Pr 2 and Sg 10, where I comÂ­
bine two factors: for Pr 2, I combine the factors of eï¬€ort and 
derived oï¬€enses to clarify the relationship between how taking 
is deï¬ned in scenarios listed in the vibh and the factors that 
aï¬€ect the severity of the oï¬€ense; for Sg 10, I combine the facÂ­
tors of procedure and derived oï¬€enses to clarify the relationÂ­
ship between the successive steps in dealing with a bhikkhu 
agitating for a schism and the oï¬€enses incurred at each stage of 
that process. Also, I summarize each rule statement rather than 
provide full English translations. Please refer to bmc1 and the 
abp, bd, pÄt, & tap for full translations of the rule statements.
Â¶This work is designed to be a supplement to bmc1. AccordÂ­
ingly, I donâ€™t explain all of the terminology used, both for the 
sake of concision and because I assume that the reader is faÂ­
miliar with most of the terminology from reading bmc1. Please 
refer to bmc1 for explanations of any terms that I donâ€™t explain.
Â¶I also encourage the reader to refer to bmc1 because it goes 
above and beyond this work in three respects:
Â¶bmc1 provides the context for many rules, i.e., the origin 
stories for the rules. These origin stories serve several funcÂ­
tions: they provide additional clues as to the intent of the rules 
as well as further considerationsâ€”such as the principles and 
models illustrated in the circumstances under which the rules 
were formulatedâ€”pertinent to the analysis of an oï¬€ense.
Â¶Understanding these aspects of the origin stories is necesÂ­
sary because the rules refer to technologies, beliefs, and pracÂ­
tices speciï¬c to the geographic and socio-historical context in 
which they were formulated. So as the rules are applied to ever-
changing geographic and socio-historical conditions, knowing 
these aspects of the rules is necessary for ï¬guring out how to 
fâ€‚|â€‚Preface


appropriately apply them to diï¬€erent circumstances.
Â¶But even in instances in which some of the technologies, beÂ­
liefs, and practices havenâ€™t changed since the time of the forÂ­
mulation of the rules, understanding these aspects of the oriÂ­
gin stories is important because they provide guidelines for 
dealing with cases that arenâ€™t speciï¬cally addressed in the 
rules. Aï¬…er all, the rules werenâ€™t designed to be an exhaustive, 
comprehensive catalogue of all of the ways that a bhikkhu 
could engage in misconduct. Rather, the Buddha established 
the rules on a case-by-case basis, in response to speciï¬c occaÂ­
sions or instances of misconduct. So the origin stories, togethÂ­
er with the rules, provide both speciï¬c and general principles 
to be applied, when appropriate, to cases and circumstances 
that arenâ€™t explicitly discussed in the rules.
Â¶bmc1 also provides arguments for and against interpretaÂ­
tions of the rules and how they are applied. As with any disÂ­
ciplinary code, the rules can be interpreted in many ways. As 
for the Vinaya, what complicates matters furtherâ€”aside from 
discrepancies among the diï¬€erent editions of the PÄá¸·i text, the 
issues of translation, and the issues around adapting the rules 
to diï¬€erent contextsâ€”is the copious amount of commentarial 
literature produced over the centuries, in many cases elucidatÂ­
ing diï¬ƒcult or ambiguous points and in some cases distortÂ­
ing the content of the Canon. In bmc1, á¹¬hÄnissaro Bhikkhu 
has taken the commentarial literature into consideration and, 
where it contradicts or elaborates beyond the scope of the CanÂ­
on, sought among the texts the points most consistent with the 
rules, principles, models, and virtues provided in the Canon. So 
the reader should refer to bmc1 to understand the reasoning 
behind the adoption of a particular interpretation of a rule.
Â¶And in the process of arguing for and against various interÂ­
pretations, bmc1 provides an example of how to think Vinaya, 
i.e., how to handle sources and adjudicate interpretations. BeÂ­
cause of the possibility of using the source material to valiÂ­
date interpretations that arenâ€™t Dhamma-Vinayaâ€”irrespective 
of context, level of authority, or internal consistency with the 
standards of Dhamma-Vinaya provided in the Canonâ€”a copiÂ­
ous amount of literature must be consulted for a thorough and 
responsible analysis of the rules: the relevant canonical literaÂ­
ture in the Vinaya and suttas, the variant PÄá¸·i editions of those 
texts, and layers on layers of commentarial literature proÂ­
duced over many centuries in several countries. In its engageÂ­
ment with this material, bmc1 not only provides arguments 
for what interpretations it thinks are most consistent with the 
Prefaceâ€‚|â€‚g


rules, principles, and models of Dhamma-Vinaya provided in 
the Canon, but also demonstrates how interpretations should 
be analyzed to come to responsible and consistent conclusions 
about what is and isnâ€™t Dhamma-Vinaya. This is important 
because thinking Vinaya is a skill, and to develop this skill, as 
with any skill, it is necessary to study examples to understand 
the principles and methodologies that should be used to sucÂ­
ceed in that skill. So I encourage the reader to approach bmc1 
as more than just descriptive but also demonstrativeâ€”so that 
the reader can successfully develop the skill of thinking Vinaya 
too.
Â¶To help the reader develop this skill, this work is aimed at 
providing the most essential information regarding both the 
content of the rules and their application. This guide is thus 
primarily aimed at providing TheravaÌ„da Buddhist monks with 
the information they need, whether as a study aid or reference, 
to clearly understand what the rules are and how they should 
be applied. Accordingly, I have added Appendices 1â€“5, which 
provide information about edibles, marking and determining 
requisites, forfeiture, confession, and penance & probation. 
I didnâ€™t include the procedures and PÄá¸·i formulas for shared 
ownership and verdicts; those interested in that information 
should refer to bmc1.
Â¶But this work is intended not only for monks but also for layÂ­
people interested in deepening their understanding of the disÂ­
cipline. Whether out of simple curiosity about the Vinaya or to 
educate themselves about the issues relevant to their interacÂ­
tions with TheravaÌ„da Buddhist monks, lay readers should ï¬nd 
in this work much of the information they need to understand 
both the application of the Vinaya in the past and present and 
its relevance to the practice of the Dhamma here and now.
Â¶It is with this in mind that I point out how the Vinaya is not, 
as some may think, some anachronistic, minutiae-obsessed 
system of rules that somehow gets in the way of what is really 
important: the practice of the Dhamma. Rather, as á¹¬hÄnissaro 
Bhikkhu points out in the Introduction to bmc1, the Vinaya is 
an inextricable part of practicing the Dhamma; the Dhamma & 
Vinaya as the Buddha taught them function together as DhamÂ­
ma-Vinaya to provide a total training of the mind for the sake 
of release.
Â¶I would also point out how, when it comes to the Vinaya, peoÂ­
ple oï¬…en confuse restraint (i.e., to control) with constraint (i.e., 
to conï¬ne, bind, fetter, or oppress). However, when properly 
observed, the Vinaya doesnâ€™t just lead to freedom through reÂ­
hâ€‚|â€‚Preface


straint: it provides freedom in restraint. This freedom is two-
fold: by restraining themselves in line with the Vinaya, monks 
not only free themselves from many of the burdens that get in 
the way of the practice but also free others from the burden of 
their unskillful actions. It is in this way that restraint becomes 
both an expression of self-control and an expression of good 
will and compassion, attitudes that underlie the practice of 
Dhamma-Vinaya as a whole and are, when properly cultivated, 
boundless, limitless attitudes that conduce to unbinding.
Â¶And the Vinaya isnâ€™t just about what monks can and canâ€™t do. 
It is the expression of disciplinary norms in various formsâ€”
as rules, principles, models, and virtuesâ€”to provide subjective 
and objective standards with which to judge which actions are 
skillful (i.e., leading to release) and which actions are unskillful 
(i.e., leading to further stress and suï¬€ering). I hope that the disÂ­
cerning reader, lay or ordained, will understand more than just 
the importance of practicing the Vinaya in accordance with the 
Vinaya, but also the relevance, to the readerâ€™s own practice of 
the Dhamma, of the principles, models, and virtues that underÂ­
lie the PÄá¹­imokkha rules.
Â¶AnumodanÄ to the following people for their contributions to 
this project: A.G. for encouraging me to write this book and for 
editing a previous version and responding to my questions; J.B. 
for his comments and support; K.B., J.L., N.L., M.B., M.L.V., A.S., 
and A.B.G. for their comments on a previous version as well; 
Ã‘.B. and S.B. for responding to my questions; and K.B. for his 
work on the type.
B.A.

NOTE
Blanket exemptions. Although bmc1 says that the four blanket 
exemptions apply to nearly all of the rulesâ€”and so it mentions 
them rarelyâ€”several of the exemptions are omitted from the 
vibhâ€™s treatment of many rules. So I have reinserted the approÂ­
priate non-oï¬€ense clauses speciï¬c to each rule in accordance 
with the vibh.
Community Â» Saá¹…gha/saá¹…gha. bmc1 translates saá¹…gha as CommuÂ­
nity and capitalizes the term throughout. I have leï¬… the term 
untranslated and capitalize it only when it refers to the BhikÂ­
khu Saá¹…gha or BhikkhunÄ« Saá¹…gha as a whole; otherwise, when 
it refers to a local monastic communityâ€”or is used as an adjecÂ­
tive to modify an act performed by a local monastic community 
or a person who performs speciï¬c duties in a local monastic 
communityâ€”I leave the term in lowercase. This distinction is 
signiï¬cant in several rules, including NP 30 & Pc 82 (in which 
diverting gains allocated for one local monastic community to 
another is a Dk), where using Community throughout would obÂ­
fuscate the distinction between the Bhikkhu Saá¹…gha and a local 
saá¹…gha, and using community would obfuscate the distinction 
between a monastic community and a community of people in 
general.
Third person Â» second person. Rather than use the third person 
pronouns the bhikkhu or one throughout, as is done in bmc1, I 
use the second person pronoun you because my primary audiÂ­
ence is bhikkhus. The exceptions are when I discuss general 
procedures or protocol (e.g., Ay 1) or when I paraphrase allowÂ­
ances in the vibh or kdks.
Blanket exemptions:

responsibility for an oï¬€ense  
if he commits it while 1) 
insane; 2) possessed by spirits;  
3) delirious with pain; or  
4) if he is the ï¬rst oï¬€ender.

ÄrÄjika (defeat)â€”The pÄrÄjikas are the 
most serious class of oï¬€enses in the Bhikkhu 
PÄá¹­imokkha. There are four pÄrÄjikas, and 
the penalty for pÄrÄjikas is that you are, 
immediately upon committing the oï¬€ense, 
no longer a bhikkhu and no longer in 
aï¬ƒliationâ€”i.e., you can no longer participate in any saá¹…gha 
meetings or the uposatha and no longer have a training in 
common with the bhikkhusâ€”and you are irrevocably barred 
from reordaining as a bhikkhu for the rest of your life. Even 
if you conceal your oï¬€ense and pretend to continue being 
a bhikkhu, you donâ€™t really count as one and as soon as your 
deceit is discovered, the other bhikkhus are duty-bound to 
expel you from the Saá¹…gha. If you try to ordain in another 
saá¹…gha that doesnâ€™t know of your oï¬€ense, your ordination is 
invalid and you must be expelled as soon as the truth is known.
Â¶Ignorance of the four pÄrÄjikas doesnâ€™t exempt you from the 
penalty. This is why the Buddha ordered that they be taught to 
each new bhikkhu immediately upon ordination (mv.1.78.2â€“5).
Â¶In addition to the four pÄrÄjikas in the Bhikkhu PÄá¹­imokkha 
and the four additional pÄrÄjikas for bhikkhunÄ«s, there are, 
according to the comm, twelve equivalent pÄrÄjikas. The twelve 
equivalent pÄrÄjikas include the eleven disqualiï¬ed types who 
shouldnâ€™t be ordained as bhikkhus. If a disqualiï¬ed person is 
ordained, his ordination is invalid and he must be expelled 
from the Saá¹…gha for life as soon as his true status is known. 
The twelve equivalent pÄrÄjikas are the following: a person who
1.	is a paá¹‡á¸aka;
2.	is a non-human (e.g., nÄgas, petas, devas, and yakkhas);
3.	is a hermaphrodite;
4.	poses as a bhikkhu without being a bhikkhu;
5.	ordains in another religion without ï¬rst giving up his staÂ­
tus as a bhikkhu;
6.	has murdered his father;
7.	has murdered his mother;
8.	has murdered an arahant;
9.	has sexually molested a bhikkhunÄ«;
10.	has maliciously caused a Buddha to bleed;
11.	has caused a schism in the Saá¹…gha, knowing or suspecting 
that his position was contrary to the Dhamma-Vinaya;

saá¹…ghÄdisesa rules are the second most serious 
class of oï¬€enses in the Bhikkhu PÄá¹­imokkha. 
There are thirteen saá¹…ghÄdisesas, and the penalty 
for saá¹…ghÄdisesas is that you are required to tell a 
suitable bhikkhu of the oï¬€ense and to ask a saá¹…gha of at least 
four bhikkhus to impose a six-day period of penance (mÄnatta) 
on you. If you commit a saá¹…ghÄdisesa and conceal it from your 
fellow bhikkhus past the following dawnrise, you must observe 
an additional period of probation (parivÄsa) for the same 
number of days as you concealed the oï¬€ense. Only aï¬…er you 
complete your probation may you ask for the six-day period 
of penance. Once you complete your penance, you must ask a 
saá¹…gha of at least twenty bhikkhus to give you rehabilitation. 
For details on concealment, penance, and probation, see app. 5.
Â¶The thirteen saá¹…ghÄdisesas are classed as heavy oï¬€enses 
(garukÄpatti), not only because of their seriousness but because 
the procedures of penance, probation, and rehabilitation are 
burdensome by design, both for the oï¬€ender and the saá¹…ghaâ€”
an added deterrent for any potential oï¬€ender.
Â¶In its discussion of Sg 10â€“13, the vibh says that â€œ[f ]or one 
who commits [the full oï¬€ense], the [Dk] with the notiï¬cation 
and the [Tcs] with the two proclamations subside,â€ and the 
commentaries derive from this a general principle that â€œwhen a 
heavier penalty is incurred â€¦ only that penalty is counted, and 
the preceding lighter ones are nulliï¬edâ€; the commentaries 
then use the Great Standards to apply this principle to all of 
the rules (abp; bmc1 64). But the Canon doesnâ€™t mention this 
passage in any other rules, which suggests that it applies only 
to Sg 10â€“13. So aside from those four rules, you must make 
amends for all of the oï¬€enses you incur in the course of 
committing an act involving multiple classes of oï¬€enses.

Building an unsponsored, plastered hut for yourself without 
saá¹…gha approval or if the hut exceeds the standard measureÂ­
ment
There are two subfactors:
1.	The hut. The hut must qualify as a hut and exceed the stanÂ­
dard measurement.
Hut. Plastered inside, outside, or both. comm: it must be 
wider than 3 sugata spans (75 cm; ~2.5 ï¬…) to count as a hut.
Plastered. comm: The vibh refers to a plastered roof. The 
plaster must be clay or white lime (bmc1: includes cement) 
and the plastering on the inside or outside of the roof must 
be contiguous with the plastering on the inside or outside 
of the walls for it to qualify as a hut.
Standard measurement. 12 sugata spans long Ã— 7 sugata spans 
wide (3 m Ã— 1.75 m; ~9.8 ï¬… Ã— ~5.7 ï¬…). The length of the hut is 
measured from the outside (comm: excluding the plasterÂ­
ing) and the width is measured from the inside. Neither of 
these measurements may be exceeded.
Sugata span. bmc1: 1 sugata span = 25 cm; ~9.8 in.
2.	Procedure. You must choose a site, clear it, and get saá¹…gha 
approval before starting construction of the hut.
a.	Choosing the site. The site must be free of disturbances 
and have adequate space.

Free of disturbances.
1)	 It isnâ€™t the living place of creatures that might harm 
the hut (e.g., termites, ants, or rats).
2)	 It isnâ€™t the living place of creatures that might harm 
the inhabitant (e.g., snakes, scorpions, or tigers).
3)	 It isnâ€™t near any places that will disturb the inhabÂ­
itantâ€™s peace and quiet (e.g., it isnâ€™t near ï¬elds, orÂ­
chards, places of execution, cemeteries, pleasure 
groves, royal property, elephant stables, horse staÂ­
bles, prisons, taverns, slaughterhouses, highways, 
crossroads, public rest-houses, or meeting places).
Adequate space. There is enough room on the site for a 
yoked wagon or a man carrying a ladder to go around 
the hut.
b.	Clearing the site. bmc1, based on the vibh: Clearing unÂ­
derbrush to determine if there are disturbances. Only 
aï¬…er the saá¹…gha approves the site should trees be 
cleared. comm: the site should be leveled.
note: bmc1: when building a hut on monastery propÂ­
erty, the wise policy is to get permission from the abbot 
before clearing the site.
c.	Getting the site inspected. The site must be inspected by 
all of the bhikkhus in the saá¹…gha (if possible) or the 
bhikkhu designated by the saá¹…gha (through a formal 
motion with one announcement) to inspect the site. The 
inspecting bhikkhu must inspect the site and determine 
if it is free of disturbances and has adequate space. If it 
doesnâ€™t qualify, he should tell you not to build there. If it 
qualiï¬es, he should tell the saá¹…gha that the site is free of 
disturbances and has adequate space.
d.	Getting the site approved. Getting saá¹…gha approval 
through a formal petition and motion. Once the motion 
has passed, you may begin construction.
For your use.
Your use. comm: to claim ownership of the hut and consider it 
yours rather than belonging to the saá¹…gha or someone else.
note: comm: includes if you build or have someone build a 
saá¹…gha building (e.g., a meeting hall) that you claim and use as 
your dwelling.
Completing a hut or getting someone else to complete it by 
begging for building materials (i.e., without a sponsor).

niyata (indeï¬nite)â€”The aniyatas are a 
class of two PÄá¹­imokkha rules that donâ€™t asÂ­
sign penalties like most other rules. Instead, 
they detail procedures by which the saá¹…gha 
may pass judgment when a bhikkhu in unÂ­
certain circumstances is accused of commitÂ­
ting an oï¬€ense.
When a trustworthy female lay follower accuses a bhikkhu of 
a Pr, Sg, or Pc while sitting alone with a female in a private, seÂ­
cluded place, the saá¹…gha should investigate the charge and deal 
with him in accordance with whatever he admits to doing
Trustworthy. bmc1, based on the vibh: She is at least a stream-
winner (sotÄpanna). If she isnâ€™t at least a stream-winner, the 
saá¹…gha may choose whether to investigate the charge, but if she 
is, they must. bmc1: the same applies to a male lay follower.
Female lay follower. A female, regardless of age, who has taken 
refuge in the Buddha, Dhamma, & Saá¹…gha.
Sitting in a private, secluded seat.
Sitting. Includes lying down.
Private. Private to the eye and ear.
Private to the eye. No one else is near enough to see you two 
wink, raise an eyebrow, or nod.
Private to the ear. No one else is near enough to hear what you 
two say in a normal voice.
Secluded seat. A seat behind a wall, a closed door, a large bush, or 
anything that would aï¬€ord enough privacy to have sex.
bmc1, based on the kdks: the following is the procedure for 
dealing with a potential oï¬€ender:
1.	If a trustworthy female lay follower sees a bhikkhu with a 
female in the above circumstances, she may tell the saá¹…gha 
and charge him on the basis of what she has seen.
2.	The bhikkhus who learn of the charge are duty-bound to 
ï¬rst question the accused in private. See Sg 8, procedure. 
There are four possible outcomes:

oï¬€ense, and undergoes the appropriate penalty, nothing

b.	If he doesnâ€™t admit to the act and the bhikkhus are conÂ­
vinced of his account, the case can be dropped.
c.	If he doesnâ€™t admit to the act but the bhikkhus arenâ€™t 
convinced of his account, they may convene a saá¹…gha 
meeting to investigate further.
d.	If he admits to the act but doesnâ€™t see the oï¬€ense, the 
bhikkhus may convene a saá¹…gha meeting to press him 
further. If he still doesnâ€™t see the oï¬€ense, they may susÂ­
pend him.
Doesnâ€™t see the oï¬€ense. He refuses to acknowledge that the 
act is an oï¬€ense (mv.9.1.3; cv.11.1.10).
3.	A saá¹…gha meeting is convened. The accuser and the accused 
must be present and both must agree to the caseâ€™s being 
heard by that particular group (if the original accuser is a 
layperson, one of the bhikkhus is to take up the charge). 
The accused states his version of events [bmc1, based on 
cv.4.14.29: and the bhikkhus arenâ€™t to take his ï¬rst stateÂ­
ment at face value]. The accused must be cross-examined by 
the other bhikkhus until they are satisï¬ed that he is telling 
the truth. The saá¹…gha then passes a verdict only in line with 
what he admits to doing (mv.9.6.1â€“4). There are three possible 
verdicts that will settle the case: the accused is
a.	found innocent;
b.	deemed insane at the time he committed the oï¬€ense 
and therefore absolved of guilt;
c.	found guilty and deserves a further punishment transÂ­
action (cv.4.14.27â€“29).
note: bmc1: Normally, a bhikkhu must ask leave of the acÂ­
cused before making an accusation. But when a trustworthy 
female (or male) lay follower accuses a bhikkhu of a Pr, Sg, 
or Pc, the accusation must be brought up in a saá¹…gha meetÂ­
ing.
note: bmc1: If the accused admits to an act that is an 
oï¬€ense but refuses to see the oï¬€ense (mv.9.1.3; cv.9.1.10), 
the case isnâ€™t settled. But this much of an admission allows 
the saá¹…gha to suspend him until he admits that it was an 
oï¬€ense and is willing to undergo the penalty.
note: bmc1, based on cv.4.8: if a verdict is reached but latÂ­
er found to be wrong, the saá¹…gha may reopen the case and 
reach a new verdict.
On suspension, see bmc2, 
chap. 20.

issaggiya pÄcittiya (forfeiture and 
confession)â€”There are 30 nissaggiya 
pÄcittiyas, divided into three chapters of 
ten. The nissaggiya pÄcittiyas are classed 
as light oï¬€enses (lahukÄpatti) and are 
called desanÄ-gÄminÄ«, meaning they can be 
cleared through confession.
Â¶But before you can confess the oï¬€ense, you must forfeit the 
item. Aside from when forfeiture must be made to a saá¹…gha 
of four or more bhikkhus (e.g., NP 18, 19, 22), you may forfeit 
the item to a single bhikkhu, to a group of two or three, or to 
a saá¹…gha. Items that you have to forfeit to a saá¹…gha (e.g., gold 
or currency) may not to be returned to you but if the item is 
allowable, it must; if it isnâ€™t returned to you, the bhikkhu(s) to 
whom it was forfeited incur a dukkaá¹­a. For violations of NP 
22 & 23, there are restrictions as to what you may do with the 
returned item, but otherwise you are free to use it as you see ï¬t. 
If you use the item before forfeiting it, you incur a dukkaá¹­a, but 
if the item gets lost, destroyed, or discarded before you forfeit 
it, you may simply confess the oï¬€ense.

Lapse of determination or shared ownership. bmc1, based on the 
Canon: the determination or shared ownership of cloth can 
lapse for the following reasons:
(for determined articles)
1.	The owner rescinds the determination.
2.	comm: the cloth develops a hole.
Size of the hole. comm: At least the size of a pinky nail. If 
thread remains across the hole, the clothâ€™s determination 
lapses only if any of the parts of the hole divided by the 
thread is the size of a pinky nail or larger.
Location of the hole. comm: the determination lapses if the 
hole is
a.	(upper or outer robe) at least 1 sugata span (25 cm; ~9.8 
in.) from the longer side and 8 sugata ï¬ngerbreadths 
(~16.6 cm; ~6.6 in) from the shorter side;
b.	(lower robe) at least 1 sugata span (25 cm; ~9.8 in.) from 
the longer side and 4 sugata ï¬ngerbreadths (~8.3 cm; ~3.3 
in.) from the shorter side.
note: comm: if you are patching an area farther than the 
speciï¬ed distance, the determination lapses if you cut out 
the worn area before applying the patch but doesnâ€™t lapse if 
you apply the patch before cutting out the worn area.
(for determined & shared articles)
3.	The owner disrobes or dies.
4.	The owner gives the cloth away.
5.	The cloth is lost, destroyed, burnt, or snatched away.
6.	Someone takes it on trust.
On trust. See Pc 59, non-offenses, #2.
(for shared articles)
7.	Either owner takes the cloth on trust or rescinds the shared 
ownership.
note: perception: if the robe-cloth isnâ€™t determined, put unÂ­
der shared ownership, abandoned, given or thrown away, lost, 
destroyed, burnt, snatched away, or taken on trust, you would 
incur the full oï¬€ense regardless of whether you perceive that 
it isnâ€™t determined, put under shared ownership, abandoned, 
given or thrown away, lost, destroyed, burnt, snatched away, or 
taken on trust or if you are in doubt.

Dawnrise. bmc1, based on the vl/sub: dawnrise corresponds to 
the beginning of civil twilight. bmc1: In Thailand, dawnrise 
is oï¬…en determined by looking at the palm of your hand as it 
is held out at full armâ€™s length: dawnrise has passed when the 
major lines of the hand are visible by natural light. On a bright, 
moonlit night, dawnrise is determined by looking at the foliage 
of trees: dawnrise has passed when you can see the green in the 
leaves.
Zone. bmc1, based on the vibh: the zone depends on the type of 
location, whether the area around the location is enclosed, and, 
if it is enclosed, whether it belongs to one or more kula (clan; 
family).
Enclosed. comm: surrounded by a wall, fence, or moat. s/comm: 
moat includes rivers and lakes.
Single kula. bmc1, based on the vibh: a building, vehicle, or parÂ­
cel of land that belongs to one family or a village governed by 
a single person. s/comm: includes monasteries â€œinitiatedâ€ by 
people belonging to one family.
Multi-kula. bmc1, based on the vibh: a building, vehicle, or parÂ­
cel of land that belongs to more than one family (bmc1: e.g., 
an apartment complex) or a village governed by a group. bmc1: 
towns governed under a social contract (e.g., a town charter) 
count as multi-kula even if the highest authority in the governÂ­
ment is invested in a single person. s/comm: includes monasÂ­
teries â€œinitiatedâ€ by people belonging to more than one family.
note: perception: abp; bd: if you are outside of the zone in 
which your basic set of robes are placed, you would incur the 
full oï¬€ense regardless of whether you perceive that you are 
outside of the zone or if you are in doubt.
note: perception: if the robe isnâ€™t released, discarded, lost, deÂ­
stroyed, burned, or stolen, you would incur the full oï¬€ense reÂ­
gardless of whether you perceive that it isnâ€™t released, discardÂ­
ed, lost, destroyed, burned, or stolen [abp; bd: or if you are in 
doubt].
Released. bmc1: you gave it away or rescinded its determination.
Zones. comm: When the vibh mentions robes kept in a certain 
area, you should be within a hatthapÄsa of the boundary of the 
area. When the vibh doesnâ€™t mention that you should be in a 
certain area, you should be within a hatthapÄsa of the robes.
HatthapÄsa. abp: â€œthis may be hattha (hand) + pa (to) + aÌ„sa (shoulÂ­
der)â€”lit. â€˜hand-to-shoulder.â€™â€

Examples:
A monastic dwelling (s/comm: includes entire monasteries):
1.	Enclosed and single-kula: if the robes are in the enclosure, 
you must be in the enclosure.
2.	Enclosed and multi-kula: you must be in the dwelling 
where the robes are placed, at the entrance to the enclosure, 
or within a hatthapÄsa of the dwelling or the entrance to 
the enclosure.
3.	Unenclosed: if the robes are in the dwelling, you must be in 
the dwelling or within a hatthapÄsa of the dwelling.
A dwelling with a yard (bmc1: e.g., a laypersonâ€™s house):
1.	Enclosed and single-kula: if the robes are in the enclosure, 
you must be in the enclosure.
2.	Enclosed and multi-kula: you must be in the room where 
the robes are placed, at the [bmc1: entrance to the enclosure; 
abp: entry-gate; bd: main entrance], or within a hatthapÄsa 
of either.
note: cbmc1, based on the vibh: it appears that the zone 
may not be contiguous, as the entrance to the enclosure 
may be distant from the room where the robes are placed.
3.	Unenclosed: you must be in the room where the robes are 
placed or within a hatthapÄsa of the room.
Buildings without a yard (bmc1: e.g., a city apartment block):
1.	Single-kula: if the robes are in the building, you must be in 
the building.
2.	Multi-kula: you must be in the room where the robes are 
placed, at the [bmc1: entrance to the building; abp: entry-
gate; bd: main entrance], or within a hatthapÄsa of either.
note: cbmc1, based on the vibh: it appears that the zone 
may not be contiguous, as the entrance to the building may 
be distant from the room where the robes are placed.
A boat (bmc1: includes other vehicles):
1.	Single-kula (bmc1: e.g., boats, rvs): if the robes are in the 
vehicle, you must be in the vehicle.
2.	Multi-kula (bmc1: e.g., trains, commercial buses, and airÂ­
planes): you must be in the room where the robes are placed 
or within a hatthapÄsa of the room [thai: or within a 
hatthapÄsa of the entrance to the boat].
A caravan (s/comm: includes groups traveling by foot [bmc1: 
e.g., hiking trips]):
1.	Single-kula: if the robes are in the caravan, you must be 
within 7 abbhantaras (98 m; ~321.5 ï¬…) of the front or back

Robe cloth. See NP 1, object.
Asking for robe-cloth from an unrelated layperson, except on a 
proper occasion.
Proper occasion. If your robes were snatched away or destroyed.
Snatched away. bmc1, based on the vibh: stolen or conï¬scated

Destroyed. Burnt, carried away by water, eaten by pests, or worn 
out with use.
Worn out. s/comm: it no longer properly covers the body.
Unrelated. See NP 4, effort.

Excessively prompting a steward about getting a requisite from 
a personal fund
bmc1, based on the vibh: a fund leï¬… with a steward to buy 
robe-cloth (comm: or any fund for any type of requisite. InÂ­
cludes funds for a single bhikkhu, a group of bhikkhus, a 
saá¹…gha, or impersonal funds [bmc1: e.g., for construction or 
book printing]).
Steward. cbmc1, based on bmc1: a layperson or entity responÂ­
sible for handling funds or transactions on behalf of a bhikkhu 
or group of bhikkhus. comm: there are three types of stewards:
1.	Indicated by the bhikkhu.
a.	The donor asks the bhikkhu who his steward is and the 
bhikkhu points the person out.
b.	The donor, knowing a layperson has volunteered to be 
a bhikkhuâ€™s steward or is on familiar terms with the 
bhikkhu, gives funds to the steward and tells the bhikÂ­
khu before or aï¬…er doing so.
2.	Indicated by the donor or messenger. The donor chooses a perÂ­
son to act as the steward for a particular giï¬… and tells the 
bhikkhu before or aï¬…er doing so.
3.	Indicated by neither.
a.	The donor asks the bhikkhu who his steward is and the 
bhikkhu says he has none. Another person overhears 
and volunteers to act as the steward for that giï¬….
b.	The donor gives funds to the steward or someone else 
on familiar terms with the bhikkhu but doesnâ€™t tell the 
bhikkhu aï¬…er doing so.
note: comm: this rule applies only to stewards indicated by 
the bhikkhu. bmc1: this rule should apply to all three types of 
stewards.
bmc1, based on the vibh: excessively prompting the steward 
about getting a requisite from the fund.
Excessive. More than three verbal promptings and six silent 
promptings.
Verbal promptings. bmc1, based on the vibh: you should only 
state your need and shouldnâ€™t command the steward to get a 
requisite from the fund (e.g., I need X is allowable whereas Get 
me X, Buy me X, or Get X in exchange for me violates NP 20).
Silent promptings (standings). bmc1, based on the vibh: When 
you merely stand in the stewardâ€™s presence. If the steward asks, 
What have you come for? you should say, You know or You should 
know.

Accepting gold or currency, having someone else accept it, or 
consenting to it being put down near you
(bmc1: this rule is best explained as covering 
two separate but related oï¬€enses)
(The ï¬rst oï¬€ense)
Gold or silver or whatever is used as currency. bmc1, based on 
the vibh: includes paper or coin currency [bmc1: and money 
orders and cashierâ€™s checks not made out to a speciï¬c payee].
Currency. bmc1: there are three requirements for any currency:
1.	They are a generally accepted medium of exchange.
2.	They are of standard recognized value.
3.	They are presentable by any bearer.
bmc1: what doesnâ€™t count as currency:
1.	Money orders and cashierâ€™s checks made out to a speciï¬c 
payee.
2.	Personal checks and travelersâ€™ checks. bmc1: Checks donâ€™t 
qualify as currency because they are simply an acknowlÂ­
edgement of a notice to a bank to provide funds for the payÂ­
ee. Only if you cash a check or command someone to cash 
one would you commit the full oï¬€ense.

Keeping an alms bowl for more than ten days without deterÂ­

Determinable. comm: if the bowl is

(27.5 in.) circumference, ~22.5 cm (8.75 in.) diameter.
Large. Twice the volume of a medium bowl.

ï¬ngerbreadths (~4.2 cm; ~1.6 in.).

Receiving it.
bmc1, based on the vibh: the following is the procedure for 
dealing with the oï¬€ense:
1.	A saá¹…gha meeting is convened, with each bhikkhu bringing 
his determined bowl.
2.	The oï¬€ender forfeits the bowl and confesses the oï¬€ense.
3.	The saá¹…gha, through one motion and one announcement

Bowl exchanger. He must be free of the four types of bias and 
know when a bowl is properly exchanged and when it isnâ€™t.
Four types of bias. Based on desire, aversion, delusion, and 
fear.
4.	The bowl exchanger takes the forfeited bowl to the senior-
most bhikkhu, who chooses which of the two bowls (his 
own or the forfeited one) he prefers.
5.	The bowl exchanger takes the bowl that remains aï¬…er the 
senior-most bhikkhuâ€™s choice and oï¬€ers it to the next most 
senior bhikkhu, who chooses which of the two he prefers.
6.	This process is repeated in order of seniority until the bowl 
exchanger presents the ï¬nal bowl to the oï¬€ender and tells 
him to determine it and care for it until it is no longer useÂ­
able.

PÄ€CITTIYA
Äcittiya 
(confession)â€”There 
are 
92 
pÄcittiyas, divided into eight chapters of ten 
and one of twelve. The pÄcittiyas are classed 
as light oï¬€enses (lahukÄpatti) and are called 
desanÄ-gÄminÄ«, meaning they can be cleared 
through confession.
Â¶As part of the confession, you not only confess the oï¬€ense 
and acknowledge that it was an oï¬€ense but you also say that 
you will be restrained (i.e., will make an honest, concerted 
eï¬€ort to avoid committing the oï¬€ense again). See app. 4 for the 
procedure and PÄá¸·i formulas for confession.
Â¶bmc1 abbreviates its treatment of the factor of perception for 
many of the pÄcittiyas. Although it provides cross-references 
to its more detailed discussion of the factor of perception 
under Pc 4, I have reinserted, in accordance with the vibh, the 
appropriate perception clauses in full for each pÄcittiya.
THE LIE CHAPTER
Lying
k/comm: to misrepresent the truth.
note: intention: bmc1: includes white lies. comm: includes lies 
meant as jokes. bmc1, based on the comm: includes exaggeraÂ­
tion or sarcasm.
Lying. To say that you have seen X when you havenâ€™t, that you 
havenâ€™t seen X when you have, or that you have seen X clearly 
when you are in doubt (bmc1, based on the vibh: this pattern 
applies to the other senses as well). k/comm: i.e., the eï¬€ort to 
make someone else know whatever you want to communicate 
based on an intention to misrepresent the truth. comm: inÂ­
cludes writing or gestures.

Insulting a bhikkhu to his face out of malice
bmc1, based on the vibh: insulting a bhikkhu to his face about 
any of the ten topics of abuse (akkosa-vatthu).
Insult. bmc1, based on the vibh: A direct gesture or statement, 
written or spoken, that is intended to humiliate someone. It 
may be phrased as sarcastic praise or as outright abuse (e.g., 
You are X).
Ten topics of abuse. bmc1, based on the vibh: remarking on the 
personâ€™s
1.	race, class, nationality (e.g., You Frenchman!);
2.	name (e.g., You really are a Dick);
3.	family or lineage (e.g., You son of a bitch);
4.	occupation (e.g., You capitalist pig!);
5.	craï¬… (e.g., What would you expect from a guy who crochets?);
6.	disease or handicap (e.g., Hey, Clubfoot!);
7.	physical characteristics (e.g., Hey, Fatty!);
8.	deï¬lements (e.g., You control freak);
9.	oï¬€enses (e.g., You liar!). bmc1, based on the vibh: includes 
insults about stream-entry (e.g., Some stream-winner you 
are!);
10.	using an abusive form of address (e.g., You ass!).
note: bmc1: the comm & s/comm say that any insulting reÂ­
mark not listed in the vibh would be grounds for a Dk, but the 
vibh deï¬nes the topics for abuse so broadly that any remark 
related to them in any way would fulï¬ll this factor.
note: bmc1: All of the insults in the vibh are about the perÂ­
son, whereas insults and verbal abuse nowadays can be comÂ­
mands (e.g., Go to hell!, F*ck oï¬€!). Based on intent, such comÂ­

the full oï¬€ense regardless of whether the bhikkhu feels insultÂ­

Training an unordained person to recite Dhamma

Â¶The answer to the ï¬rst question is that, in its discussion

sundown is consistently used to refer to the period between 
sundown and dawn; nowhere in the Canon is it used to reÂ­
fer to a twenty-four-hour period.
Â¶The answer to the second question is that the vibh doesnâ€™t 
explicitly deï¬ne night in this rule. But there are clues in the 
vibhâ€™s treatment of this rule and other rules that indicate 
what night means. One clue is the non-oï¬€ense clause for 
this rule that states that â€œaï¬…er staying for two nights and 
leaving before dawn [at the end] of the third night, one 
stays again,â€ which indicates that the end of the period deÂ­
noted by night is dawn (abp). This is further supported by 
the vibhâ€™s statement in this rule that nights are counted by 
dawnsâ€”i.e., that dawn is the marker for when a night has 
passed. These points are consistent with the vibh to other 
rules such as NP 2, NP 29, Pc 49, & Pc 50, where the end of 
a night is consistently referred to as dawn and nights are 
consistently counted by dawns.
Â¶Another clue as to the vibhâ€™s deï¬nition of night is a non-
oï¬€ense clause under Pc 38, which states that â€œif, having 
stored (food to be eaten) during a watch of the night, he eats

to the period between sundown and dawn, it is evident that 
night is referring to the period between sundown and dawn 
and not to a twenty-four hour period (bd 2.339â€“40; bd(h&b) 
764). In fact, nowhere in the vibh is night used to refer to a 
twenty-four hour period; it is consistently used to refer to 
the period between sundown and dawn.

THE LIVING PLANT CHAPTER
Intentionally damaging a living plant
A living plant (bhÅ«tagÄma).
BhÅ«tagÄma. comm: vegetation from
1.	bulbs, rhizomes, or tubers (bmc1: e.g., potatoes, tulips);
2.	cuttings or stakes (bmc1: e.g., willows, rose bushes);
3.	joints (bmc1: e.g., sugar-cane, bamboo);
4.	runners (bmc1: e.g., strawberries, couch grass);
5.	seeds (bmc1: e.g., corn, beans).
note: comm: A whole plant or part of one that is removed from 
its original place is no longer bhÅ«tagÄma. If it can grow again 
when put in soil, it is classed as bÄ«jagÄma (home of a seed) and 
doesnâ€™t become bhÅ«tagÄma again until the ï¬rst new shoot is a 
fresh green color and the ï¬rst new leaf appears.
BÄ«jagÄma. comm: includes mushrooms that still have their 
spores, fungi, molds, and lichens without leaves.
Damaging it (e.g., cutting, breaking, cooking) or getting someÂ­
one to do so.
Damaging. comm: includes shaking a tree limb to get the dry

A bed, bench, mattress, or stool belonging to the saá¹…gha.
Bed (maÃ±ca). abp: there are four kinds: long, slatted, with carved

Having it put away. bmc1, based on the vibh: you are responÂ­

until the visiting bhikkhus claim their seats (then the visitors 
are responsible for them). When there is a series of Dhamma 
talks, each speaker is responsible for the sermon seat from 
when he sits in it until the next speaker sits in it.

The eight rules of respect. bmc1, based on the vibh: the eight 
rules of respect are the following:
1.	Even a bhikkhunÄ« who has been ordained for over a century 
must pay homage to a bhikkhu ordained that very day.
2.	A bhikkhunÄ« must not spend the Rains in a place where 
there is no bhikkhu [comm: within a half-yojana (8 km; ~5 
miles)].
3.	Every half-month a bhikkhunÄ« should expect two things 
from the bhikkhu saá¹…gha: permission to ask the date of the 
PÄá¹­imokkha recitation and permission to approach for an 
exhortation.
4.	At the end of the Rains, every bhikkhunÄ« should invite acÂ­
cusations from the bhikkhu saá¹…gha and the bhikkhunÄ« 
saá¹…gha.
5.	A bhikkhunÄ« who has broken any of the rules of respect 
must undergo penance (mÄnatta) for a half-month under 
both saá¹…ghas.
6.	A woman may ordain as a bhikkhunÄ« only aï¬…er she, as a 
trainee (sikkhamÄnÄ), has observed the ï¬rst six of the ten 
precepts without lapse for two full years.
7.	A bhikkhunÄ« isnâ€™t to insult or revile a bhikkhu in any way 
(comm: i.e., she isnâ€™t to insult a bhikkhu about any of the ten 
topics of abuse [see Pc 2] or any other matter and she isnâ€™t to 
threaten a bhikkhu with harm).
8.	A bhikkhunÄ« may not instruct a bhikkhu, although a bhikÂ­
khu may instruct a bhikkhunÄ« (comm: i.e., a bhikkhunÄ« may 
not give commands to a bhikkhu on how to behave but she 
may teach a bhikkhu indirectly [e.g., In the past, the great 
bhikkhus behaved like this]).
note: perception: abp; bd: if you werenâ€™t validly authorized,

doubtâ€”and regardless of whether the group of bhikkhunÄ«s is

Eating a group meal
A group meal.
Group meal. A meal, consisting of any staple food, to which four 
or more bhikkhus were invited. cv.6.21.1: There are four types 
of group meals:
1.	Designated meals. bmc1, based on the comm: when only the 
number of bhikkhus invited is speciï¬ed.
2.	Invitational meals. bmc1, based on the comm: when speciï¬c, 
individual bhikkhus are invited (bmc1: e.g., Ven. X and four 
of his friends or The ï¬ve of you).
3.	Lottery meals. bmc1, based on the comm: when the bhikkhus 
are chosen by lot.
4.	bmc1: periodic meals. â€œIf it is [food given] on a day of the 
waxing or waning of the moon, if it is [given] on an ObserÂ­
vance day, it is [given] on the day aï¬…er an Observance dayâ€ 
(bd.2.313; bd(h&b) 739â€“40). bmc1, based on the comm: when 
meals are oï¬€ered at regular intervals and bhikkhus are sent 
on a rotating basis.
note: bmc1, based on the vibh, cv.6.21.1: this factor is fulï¬lled 
only by an invitational meal for four or more bhikkhus.
note: bmc1, based on the vibh, kdks: a meal designator (i.e., 
a saá¹…gha oï¬ƒcial in charge of meal distribution) is responsiÂ­
ble for designating the bhikkhus for designated meals, lottery 
meals, and periodic meals.
note: perception: if it is a group meal, you would incur the full 
oï¬€ense regardless of whether you perceive that it is a group 
meal or if you are in doubt.

note: bmc1: this factor would be fulï¬lled regardless of whethÂ­
er the bhikkhus eat the group meal together or separately.

Consuming an intoxicant
Alcohol or fermented liquor.
Alcohol. Any alcoholic beverage made from grain, yeast, or any 
combination of ingredients (bmc1: e.g., whiskey, beer, vodka, or 
gin).
Fermented liquor. Any alcoholic beverage made from ï¬‚owers, 
fruits, honey, sugar, or any combination of ingredients (bmc1: 
e.g., wine, mead, or rum).
note: bmc1: Includes any intoxicant that destroys a personâ€™s

Coï¬€ee, tea, tobacco, and betel donâ€™t have such an eï¬€ect and 
shouldnâ€™t be included.

oï¬€ense regardless of whether you perceive that it is an intoxiÂ­
cant or if you are in doubt.

A new robe (i.e., an unmarked robe that is made of allowable

Robe. comm: a completed, dyed robe that can be worn over the

2.	Shape. comm: Only round marks in at least one corner of

3.	Size. It may be as small as the tip of a blade of grass. comm: 
no smaller than a bedbugâ€™s back and no larger than the iris

Deliberately killing an animal
A living animal [bmc1, based on mv.6: visible to the naked eye].
Perceiving it as a living animal.
Having willed, having made the decision knowingly and conÂ­
sciously.
Having willed. comm: having willed, having planned, with a 
murderous intention.

mind-state, â€œcrushingâ€ through the power of an attack.

animal of life.
note: bmc1, based on the vibh: this factor is fulï¬lled only 
when you act on a clear and conscious decision to kill it.

254â€‚|â€‚Skï»¿â€†â€”â€†Introduction
ekhiya (to be trained in)â€”There are 75 sekhiyas, 
divided into four groups: proper behavior when in 
inhabited areas, when accepting and eating almsÂ­
food, when teaching Dhamma, and when urinatÂ­
ing, defecating, and spitting.
Â¶The rules themselves donâ€™t impose a penalty, but 
the vibh says that a bhikkhu who breaks any of them out of 
disrespect incurs a dukkaá¹­a. The vibh also says that you donâ€™t 
incur a penalty for violating any of them unintentionally, 
unthinkingly, unknowingly, or, in many cases, when there are 
dangers or when you are ill.
Â¶The comm adds that unknowingly doesnâ€™t mean not knowing 
the rule. For a bhikkhu not to make the eï¬€ort to know the 
rules, it says, qualiï¬es as disrespect. In other words, unknowingly 
in the non-oï¬€ense clause means knowing the rule but being 
unaware that circumstances have arisen that violate the rule. 
For example, if you are in an inhabited area and are unaware 
that your robes no longer cover your knees, you wouldnâ€™t violate 
Sk 1 or 2.
Â¶Unlike bmc1, which combines its discussion of a number of 
sekhiyas (Sk 1â€“24, 61, 62), I discuss each rule separately. Also 
unlike bmc1, which doesnâ€™t separate out the relevant factors 
in its discussion of each sekhiya, I discuss most of the relevant 
factors separately. And where bmc1 translates gamissÄmÄ« ti (I 
shall go) as I will walk/stand, I follow the abp, bd, tap, & pÄt by 
using I will go.

the knees (i.e., covering the â€œthree circlesâ€). comm: when you 
are standing, the lower edge should be no more than 8 sugata

dhikaraá¹‡a-samatha (the settling of isÂ­
sues)â€”The adhikaraá¹‡a-samathas are a class 
of seven PÄá¹­imokkha rules detailing prinÂ­

types of issues mentioned under Pc 63:
1.â€‚ Dispute issues (vivÄdÄdhikaraá¹‡a). Issues 
related to disagreements over Dhamma & 
Vinaya that the saá¹…gha must deal with by declaring which 
side is right.
2.	 Accusation-issues (anuvÄdÄdhikaraá¹‡a). Issues related to 
accusations about oï¬€enses that a saá¹…gha must deal with 
by judging whether the accusations are true.
3.	 Oï¬€ense-issues (ÄpattÄdhikaraá¹‡a). Issues related to the 
commission of oï¬€enses that a saá¹…gha must deal with by 
having the oï¬€ender undergo the appropriate penalty (e.g., 
confession, penance, or expulsion).
4.	 Duty-issues (kiccÄdhikaraá¹‡a). Saá¹…gha transactions that a 
saá¹…gha must perform properly (e.g., an ordination or the 
PÄá¹­imokkha recitation).

in cv.6. These procedures are also discussed under Pc 79 & 80

A face-to-face verdict should be given

A.	 Staple food
BhojanÄ«ya. bmc1: staple food; tap: cooked food; abp: edibles; bd;

in India at that time and so do not always ï¬t into this categoryâ€

is invalid for the following reasons:
a.	To argue that gelatin should be classed as oil because

App. 1â€†â€”â€†Tonicsâ€‚|â€‚297
paÌ„raÌ„jikaÌ„â€ƒ 1

overrides process & the classiï¬cation of its source (renÂ­
dering from â€˜ï¬‚eshâ€™). bmc1 demonstrates this principle 
of composition as a crucial criteria for the classiï¬cation 
of an edible with its argument for why cheese should be 
classed as food rather than as a tonic under butter (see 
bmc1 263). And as for the irrelevance of the classiï¬cation 
of the source of an edible in determining how an edible 
should be classed, two examples are butter and ghee: 
just because butter and ghee are derived from milk and 
milk is classed as food doesnâ€™t mean butter and ghee 
should be classed as food; rather, it is because of butter 
and gheeâ€™s composition that they are classed as tonics 
(except in the context of Pc 39).
b.	The argument for classifying gelatin as food applies the 
Great Standards based on a canonical precedent: gelaÂ­
tin, as a protein, is similar in composition to other parts 
of the body that are proteins, such as meat; and since 
meat is classed by the vibh as food under ï¬‚esh, the Great 
Standards can be applied to classify gelatin as food 
under ï¬‚esh. In contrast, bmc1â€™s argument for classifyÂ­
ing gelatin as a tonic under oil from tallow applies the 
Great Standards based on a commentarial precedent. 
And based on the comm to Pr 1â€™s statement about the 
four levels of authority of Vinaya, a valid, coherent apÂ­
plication of the Great Standards based on a canonical 
precedent is a higher authority than an application of 
the Great Standards based on a commentarial precedent 
(see bmc1 15).
c.	bmc1 states that gelatin, â€œon its own â€¦ does not serve

1)	 Gelatin is nutritionally more substantial than oil: 7

grams of protein; it is a substantial enough source of 
protein that people with diet and health concernsâ€”
especially those who want a protein source without 
fat or cholesterol and few caloriesâ€”consume it as a 
protein supplement. See the Encyclopedia of Food and 
Culture.
2)	 There is no valid, textual basis for bmc1â€™s criterion 
that an edible must serve as substantial food â€œon its 
ownâ€ for it to be classed as food. If this were a valid 
criterion, then classifying tallow as food would be

D.	 Rescinding a determination
If you want to replace your alms bowl, sitting cloth, or any one 
of your basic set of robes, you must ï¬rst rescind the determinaÂ­
tion of the old item before determining the new item unless

E.	 Determination lapses
If an item is been abandoned, given or thrown away, lost, deÂ­
stroyed, burnt, snatched away, or taken on trust, its determinaÂ­
tion automatically lapses and there is no need to rescind the 
determination before determining a replacement item.
Determination lapse. For alms-bowls, see NP 21, object, #5. For

1.	The confessant approaches the acknowledger.

a.	be experienced and competent;

sant is confessing unless the acknowledger has already

c.	bmc1: be of the same aï¬ƒliation (bmc1, based on the 
vibh: and part of the saá¹…gha [i.e., he doesnâ€™t belong to a

knowledger is a] senior monk.
4.	Both bhikkhus kneel before each other and aÃ±jali (raise the 
hands palm-to-palm in respect).

6.	The confessant forfeits the item to the acknowledger.

8.	The acknowledger recites the statement for returning the 
item and returns the forfeited item (if appropriate) to the 
confessant.

APPENDIX 4: CONFESSION
A.	 Procedure
cv 4.14.30: the procedure for confession is the following:
1.	The confessant approaches the acknowledger.
2.	The confessant and acknowledger arrange their upper 
robes over one shoulder.
Acknowledger. The acknowledger must
a.	be experienced and competent;
b.	kdks: not be guilty of the same oï¬€ense that the confeÂ­
sant is confessing unless the acknowledger has already 
confessed the oï¬€ense. If the acknowledger is guilty of 
the same oï¬€ense and hasnâ€™t confessed it, the confessant 
must confess the oï¬€ense to another bhikkhu;
c.	bmc1: be of the same aï¬ƒliation (bmc1, based on the 
vibh: and part of the saá¹…gha [i.e., he doesnâ€™t belong to a 
schismatic faction and hasnâ€™t been suspended]).
Aï¬ƒliation. See Sg 10, object, #1.
3.	The confessant bows to the [cbmc1: acknowledger if the acÂ­
knowledger is a] senior monk.
4.	Both bhikkhus kneel before each other and aÃ±jali (raise the 
hands palm-to-palm in respect).
5.	The confessant recites the confession statement.

APPENDIX 5Â­: PENANCE & PROBATION
A.	 Penance
Restrictions. bmc1, based on the kdks: A bhikkhu who comÂ­
mits a Sg is required to observe a six-day period of penance 
(mÄnatta), during which he is partially stripped of seniority and 
must observe ninety-four restrictions (cv.2.5â€“6), discussed in 
detail in bmc2, chap. 19. The four most important are that
1.	cv.2.1.4: he must not live under the same roof as a bhikkhu 
in regular standing;
2.	cv.11.7: he must live in a monastery with at least four bhikÂ­
khus in regular standing;
3.	cv.2.1.3: if he goes outside of the monastery, he must be acÂ­
companied by at least four bhikkhus in regular standing 
[bmc1: and of the same aï¬ƒliation] unless
a.	he is going to escape dangers;
b.	he is going to another place where there are bhikkhus 
in regular standing and of the same aï¬ƒliation and he 
can reach it in one day.
Aï¬ƒliation. See Sg 10, object, #1.
4.	cv.2.1.2: he must tell all of the bhikkhus in the monastery 
every day that he is observing penance and the oï¬€ense for 
which it was imposed. If visiting bhikkhus come to the 
monastery, he must tell them as well. If he goes to another 
monastery, he must tell all of the bhikkhus there.
Â¶cv.11.7: If the oï¬€ender neglects to observe any of these four 
restrictions on any day of his penance, that day doesnâ€™t count 
toward the total of six. He also incurs a Dk each time he fails to 
observe any of the ninety-four restrictions.
Rehabilitation. bmc2, based on the kdks: Once the bhikkhu has 
completed his penance, he may ask a saá¹…gha of at least twenty 
bhikkhus to give him rehabilitation. Once rehabilitated, he is 
again in regular standing.
B.	 Probation
Concealment. bmc2, based on the kdks: If a bhikkhu who comÂ­
mits a Sg conceals it from his fellow bhikkhus past the followÂ­
ing dawn, he must observe an additional period of probation 
(parivÄsa) for the same number of days as he concealed the

oï¬€ense. Only aï¬…er he has completed his probation may he then 
ask for the six-day period of penance.
Â¶bmc1: The comm (based on cv.3.23.6, cv.3.30, cv.3.34.2) sets the 
factors of concealment at ten, which may be arranged in ï¬ve 
pairs as follows:
1.	He has committed a Sg and perceives it as an oï¬€ense (i.e., 
this factor is fulï¬lled even if he thinks it is a lesser oï¬€ense).
2.	He hasnâ€™t been suspended and perceives that he hasnâ€™t been 
suspended (bmc2: a bhikkhu under suspension canâ€™t accost 
other bhikkhus so he wouldnâ€™t be able to tell them until 
aï¬…er his suspension has been liï¬…ed).
3.	There are no obstacles (e.g., a ï¬‚ood, forest ï¬re, or dangerous 
animals) and he perceives that there are no obstacles.
4.	He is able to tell another bhikkhu and he perceives that he 
is able to tell another bhikkhu.
Able to tell another bhikkhu. comm: the oï¬€ender isnâ€™t too weak 
or too ill to go and a suitable bhikkhu lives near enough 
that he may be reached that same day.
Suitable bhikkhu. comm: a bhikkhu of common aï¬ƒliation, in 
regular standing, and not on uncongenial terms with the 
oï¬€ender.
Common aï¬ƒliation. See Sg 10, object, #1.
Regular standing. bmc1: not undergoing penance, probation, 
or suspension.
5.	The oï¬€ender desires to conceal the oï¬€ense and conceals it.
What isnâ€™t concealment. bmc1, based on the comm: If the bhikÂ­
khu doesnâ€™t fulï¬ll all ten factors for concealment, he doesnâ€™t 
incur any penalty for not telling another bhikkhu that day. The 
following examples are scenarios that wouldnâ€™t qualify as conÂ­
cealment:
1.	A bhikkhu doesnâ€™t suspect that he has committed an oï¬€ense 
and realizes only much later, aï¬…er reading or hearing about 
the rules in more detail, that he has incurred a Sg.
2.	A bhikkhu lives alone in a forest and commits a Sg in the 
middle of the night. Afraid of snakes or other wild animals 
he might encounter in the dark, he waits until daylight beÂ­
fore going to tell a suitable bhikkhu.
3.	A bhikkhu lives alone in a forest and the only other bhikÂ­
khu within one dayâ€™s travel time is a personal enemy who 
will use the information to smear his nameâ€”so he travels 
another day or two to reach a suitable bhikkhu.
